Low-energy mechanisms can produce substan-\ntial injury. The response of older adults to injury is influenced by\npreexisting injuries and frailty. Medications and organ dysfunc-\ntion can alter the presentation of shock. INTRODUCTION\nGlobally, the demographic shi\" toward an aging population is\nnotable, with older adults becoming the fastest-growing age group. By 2050, it is projected that nearly half of the worldâ€™s population\nwill reside in countries where at least 20% are over 60 years old,\nwith a quarter living in countries where older adults make up\nmore than 30% of the populace. This demographic shi\" represents a significant societal change\nin the 21st century, highlighted by the increased activity levels and\nlongevity of todayâ€™s older adults compared to previous generations. Access to high-quality healthcare in industrialized regions further\nenhances their quality of life. However, the active lifestyles and\nincreased mobility of the current older adult population also\nexpose them to a greater risk of sustaining serious injuries, making\ninjury the fi\"h leading cause of death among this group. The economic status of older adults a$ects their health, and the\nhealth of geriatric patients a$ects the economy. Older adults with\nlower incomes are more likely to have disabilities and die younger. For those with lower incomes, disability is likely to begin earlier\nin life, further increasing the risk of early mortality. The social\ndeterminants of health play a key role in the health trajectory of\nolder adults. Older adults have greater healthcare and long-term\ncare needs than younger people, leading to increased healthcare\nexpenditures. 192\nAdvanced Trauma Life SupportÂ® | 11th Edition\nManaging geriatric trauma patients presents distinct\nchallenges for trauma care teams. The injury mechanisms in\nolder adults differ, with falls being more prevalent, followed\nclosely by motor vehicle and motorcycle crashes. Inadequate\ntriage of critically injured geriatric patients may contribute to their\nhigher mortality rates compared to younger individuals. Beyond\ntriage issues, the aging of organ systems, preexisting medical\nconditions, and frailty significantly elevate the mortality risk in\nolder adults, underscoring that age alone cannot predict recovery\npotential. Mental health issues, substance abuse, and the risk of\nmaltreatment are additional considerations. Achieving the best outcomes for geriatric trauma patients\ndemands a comprehensive, multidisciplinary approach focused\non meticulous initial assessment, e$ective resuscitation, and\ncontinuous care. This strategy emphasizes the need for heightened\nvigilance and tailored interventions to address the unique\nvulnerabilities of the aging population in the trauma setting. EFFECTS OF AGING\nAging is inherently associated with a decline in cellular\nfunction, leading to diminished organ performance. This\ndecline is marked by a decrease in the bodyâ€™s ability to adapt and\nmaintain homeostasis, resulting in a heightened vulnerability\nto injury-related stress, a phenomenon o\"en referred to as\n\"reduced physiological reserve\" (ï® Table 12-1). Consequently,\ninjuries that younger individuals might easily recover from can\nhave severe, sometimes catastrophic, outcomes in older adults. Research has documented the significant impact of preexisting\nconditions (PECs) on the morbidity and mortality outcomes of\ntrauma patients. A notable study identified five critical PECsâ€”\ncirrhosis, coagulopathy, chronic obstructive pulmonary disease,\nischemic heart disease, and diabetes mellitusâ€”that significantly\ninfluence trauma patient outcomes. In an analysis involving over\n3,000 patients, it was found that one in four individuals aged 65\nor older had at least one of these conditions. Furthermore, the\npresence of one or more of the critical PECs nearly doubled the\nmortality risk compared to patients without such conditions. The\nstudy also highlighted the interplay between injury severity and\nhost factors, including age, gender, and PECs, underscoring\nthat while injury severity is a primary mortality determinant,\nhost factors are also crucial (ï® Figures 12-1A and B).",

A\nsimple clinical risk nomogram to predict mortality-associated geriatric\ncomplications in severely injured geriatric patients. J Trauma Acute Care\nSurg. 2013;74(4):1125-1132; permission conveyed through Copyright\nClearance Center, Inc. The concept of frailty in the older adult, characterized by\na diminished response to physiological stress due to lower\nphysiological reserve and multiorgan system insu%ciency, has\nbeen recognized for decades and has more recently been applied\nto the trauma population. The development of a Trauma-Specific\nFrailty Index (ï® Figure 12-2) has enabled healthcare clinicians\nto identify patients at greater risk of poor outcomes and with\nincreased resource needs. Research indicates that targeted\ninterventions for these frail geriatric patients can lead to improved\noutcomes, emphasizing the importance of a tailored approach in\nthe management of geriatric trauma patients. 194\nAdvanced Trauma Life SupportÂ® | 11th Edition\nMECHANISM OF INJURY\nOlder adults are experience common mechanisms of injury\nsuch as motor vehicle crash (MVC), being struck as a pedestrian,\nburns, and penetrating injuries. Additionallyâ€”and somewhat\nunique to older adultsâ€”is the frequency of injury from ground-\nlevel falls, which can result in severe injury despite a seemingly\ntrivial mechanism. FALLS\nThe likelihood of experiencing a fall increase with age, and\nfalls are the leading cause of fatal injury among older adults. Many factors heighten the risk of falls, including older age, physical\nlimitations, a history of falls, the use of certain medications,\ndementia, balance issues, and gait instability, as well as visual,\ncognitive, and neurological impairments. Environmental hazards\nsuch as loose rugs, inadequate lighting, and slippery or irregular\nflooring also contribute significantly to the risk. Falls can also\nresult from syncope or occult infection. Despite the seemingly minor nature of ground-level\nfalls, they can inflict severe, life-threatening injuries in\nolder adults due to underlying frailty, osteoporosis, existing\nchronic conditions, and the use of certain medications such\nas anticoagulants. Traumatic brain injuries (TBI) stand out\nas a common injury from falls among older adults. Fi\"y-one\npercent of TBIs in older adults are caused by falls, while MVCs\ncause 9%. Additionally, falls leading to fractures, especially\nhip fractures, frequently result in a significant reduction of\nindependence for nearly half of the geriatric patients affected. MOTOR VEHICLE\/MOTORCYCLE COLLISION\nMVCs and motorcycle collisions (MCCs) rank as the second-\nand third-most common causes of injuries among older adults. Typically, older individuals drive fewer miles and at lower speeds\ncompared to younger drivers. However, the risk of involvement\nin MVCs and MCCs for older drivers is increased by factors such\nas slower reaction times, increased size of blind spots, reduced\nneck mobility, diminished hearing, and cognitive decline. Medical conditions like myocardial infarction, stroke, and\ndysrhythmias also contribute to scenarios that may lead to a\ncollision.",

SECURITY, COMMUNICATION, AND\nINFRASTRUCTURE\nThe tactical situation in any constrained environment is highly\ndynamic, resulting in varying degrees of threat. Both internal and\nexternal security concerns must be considered for the protection\nof both sta$ and patients. Measures may need to include increased\nphysical plant security with armed personnel or police presence\ndepending on the environment and situation; additional\nrestrictions in facility access; screening and identity verification\nof sta$, patients, and visitors; and the searching of vehicles and\npersonnel for weapons. Depending upon the environment, key\ninfrastructure considerations, such as electrical power, lighting,\nand communications, may also dramatically influence a facilityâ€™s\nsecurity posture. While these security needs will be most apparent\nin times of armed conflict, care must be taken to ensure that every\ntreatment facilityâ€™s operational plans fully address other scenarios,\nsuch as when a local facility is overwhelmed or incapacitated by\nnatural disaster, riot, or intentional mass casualty event. Likewise, reliable internal and external communication\nremains a vexing problem. Lack of system interoperability and\nreliance on native infrastructure such as vulnerable telephone\nlandlines, computer networks, or cell phone systems are frequent\ncommunication limitations. Unfortunately, failed or disrupted\ncommunications remain a frequent issue in military, disaster,\nand rural environments, and contingency plans must be\nestablished in advance. During times of war, movement of casualties from point\nof injury to initial care and between medical facilities may be\nhindered by the security environment or other constraints such\nas distance, weather, or lack of available assets. This may lead to\nprolonged periods of care at various locations throughout the\ncontinuum and may be overwhelming to smaller facilities at\ntimes. Additionally, there is o\"en attrition of medical forces, loss\nof medical infrastructure, and destruction of medical resources\ndue to either intentional targeting or proximity to other battlefield\ntargets. SPECIAL POPULATIONS\nSpecial populations during times of conflict include civilians\nwithin the area of conflict who may be injured or displaced. Children are particularly at risk for injury and illness. Injuries\nto these populations may be both physical and psychological. Additionally, sexual- and gender-based violence is o\"en more\nprevalent during times of conflict. Further complicating this\nsituation is the disruption of societal infrastructure and potential\nloss of healthcare facilities. High-energy\ngunshot wounds, such as those from assault rifles, are created by\nthe linear and cavitating (radial) energy of the missile and cause\ntissue devitalization and destruction outside the actual path of the\nmissile. High-energy explosives are commonly found in military\nmunitions or in the commercial-grade explosives o\"en seen in\nimprovised explosive devices (IEDs). While IEDs are most o\"en\nencountered in theaters of war, they are also increasingly utilized\nas a weapon of choice for intentional mass casualty events at home\nand abroad. Mortality and morbidity depend upon the distance\nfrom the device, extent of available protection, and proximity to\nimmediate medical support. It is the fluidity and potential chaos inherent to the austere\nenvironment that dictate the importance of the Zero Survey in\npractice. Triage decisions and initial care priorities may change\nrapidly as the situational factors and care capacity of the facility\nevolve over time and between events. In this environment, as\npersonnel and supply resources become more limited, triage\ndecisions become increasingly di%cult. QUATERNARY SURVEY\nAlthough the standard ATLS Course emphasizes preparation of\nthe injured patient for transfer from the initial facility to a trauma\ncenter, this is typically a single transfer over a relatively short\ndistance by a fully equipped medical team. In contrast, a patient\nin the operational environment may undergo multiple sequential\ntransfers over prolonged distances, while initial resuscitation is\nongoing. It is not uncommon to have a patient undergo a major\ndamage control surgery and then be placed into the medical\nevacuation continuum within minutes to hours of surgery and\/or\ninjury. These transfers may be via helicopter, ground ambulance,\nor even watercra\", making ongoing, enroute care exceedingly\nchallenging. Therefore, strict attention must be paid to the\ncomplete preparation of the patient for safe transportation in\norder to minimize the likelihood of problems or complications\narising during transport. The Quaternary Survey formalizes this preparation for\ntransfer. It should be repeated for each successive transfer in the\nmedical evacuation chain. In the operational setting, the time in\ntransit may be a matter of minutes or may be many hours, and this\nmust also be factored into not only the preparation for transport\nbut also the decision about readiness for transport. Enroute care\ncapabilities must also be considered, because they may vary widely\nin terms of the transportation platform, available enroute care\nproviders, equipment, supplies and medications, environment,\nand the potential for external threats. Assessing the patientâ€™s response to resuscitation is critical. The\npotential of meeting desirable end points of resuscitation versus\nthe local resources to meet these end points are real and important\nconsiderations. Although it is certainly desirable to ensure that a\nseverely injured patient is clinically stable, has had a complete and\nthorough evaluation with identification of all injuries, and has\nbeen fully resuscitated to standard end points, this is o\"en not\nCOURSE MANUAL | Chapter 24 | Injury in Combat Zones and Austere Environments\npractical or possible in the operational environment. The limited\nsupply of critical resources such as blood products, as well as the\nlimited holding capacity of the most forward medical treatment\nfacilities (such as the Forward Surgical Team), make prolonged\ncare and sustained massive transfusions logistically impossible. Thus, o\"en the best of two suboptimal choices must be made, and\nthe patient is placed into the transport system at a much earlier\nphase or in a more tenuous point of their resuscitation than is\nfrequently done in the civilian setting. Additional considerations as patients are moved in the\noperational environment include the following:\nâ€¢ Will weather or hostile action prevent movement of casualties?\nâ€¢ What supportive treatments must accompany the patient\n(ventilator, suction), and what are the potential enroute\nproblems or malfunctions that could occur?\nâ€¢ Will the evacuation team have the skills to manage a critically\nill patient and the supportive equipment accompanying the\npatient?\nâ€¢ What medications, fluids, blood products, and other\nresuscitative or supportive treatments can realistically and\nreliably be administered during the transport?\nâ€¢ What protective equipment is needed to prevent hypothermia,\neye injury, and ear\/hearing injury during transport? One must\nmaintain a hypervigilant index of suspicion for internal damage,\nincluding vascular injuries, because high-energy blasts o\"en result\nin a complex combination of wounds, ranging from devastating\ntraumatic amputation to multiple small penetrating wounds\nwith highly variable penetration and wound trajectories that are\nextremely di%cult to assess without adjunct imaging. RADIATION AND CHEMICAL AGENTS\nRadiological and chemical agents may also be involved in\nconflict, industrial accidents, terrorist attacks, or as a consequence\nof natural disasters, such as at Fukushima. Deployed medical units\nmust provide means to detect these agents and decontaminate\npatients as needed prior to assessing and performing initial\nmanagement of associated injuries. The US Department of Health\nand Human Services Administration for Strategic Preparedness\nand Responseâ€™s Radiation Emergency Medical Management\n(REMM) and Chemical Hazards Emergency Medical\nManagement (CHEMM) websites and apps are good planning\nresources for radiation and chemical exposure management. Further complicating matters is the fact that when these agents\nare used in warfare, the injury pattern o\"en includes not only\nthe insult from the radiation or toxin but also other aspects of\npolytrauma and burn injury. Depending upon the toxin and\ndegree of exposure, this may impact the anticipated prognosis and\ntreatment options. SYSTEM CONSIDERATIONS\nJOINT TRAUMA SYSTEM AND TACTICAL\nCOMBAT CASUALTY CARE\nMortality from combat wounds steadily improved over the\ncourse of the wars in Iraq and Afghanistan despite an increase\nin Injury Severity Scores. This trend stems from improvements\nin protective equipment, establishment of a system of in-theater\ntrauma care, use of tourniquets, application of damage control\nsurgery and resuscitation, and use of long-range critical care\ntransport for the most severely injured. This was only possible due\nto the widespread implementation of Tactical Combat Casualty\nCare (TCCC) and the establishment of the US Department of\nDefenseâ€™s Joint Trauma System (JTS), which supports trauma\nreadiness and outcomes through evidence-driven performance\nimprovement. Today, through maintenance of the Department\nof Defense Trauma Registry, JTS Clinical Practice Guidelines,\nand TCCC guidelines, the JTS ensures high-quality trauma care\nthroughout the US militaryâ€™s globally deployed trauma system. Additional resources including guidelines for deployed trauma\ncare can be found on the JTS website (https:\/\/jts.health.mil\/). 330\nAdvanced Trauma Life SupportÂ® | 11th Edition\nA precedent for the modification of civilian trauma training\ncourses to incorporate military-specific needs can be found\nin the example of Prehospital Trauma Life Support (PHTLS)\nand TCCC. Initially developed as a curriculum for US Special\nOperations Command, TCCC has now been implemented across\nthe battlefield and is the standard for combat prehospital care. A\nMilitary Edition of the PHTLS textbook was developed to help\nsupport this curriculum. The widespread implementation and\ntraining of all combat personnel in TCCC as competent initial\nresponders has resulted in demonstrable reductions in preventable\ndeath on the battlefield. TCCC divides point of injury care into three distinct phases: 1. Care Under Fire\n2. Tactical Field Care\n3. Tactical Evacuation\nCARE UNDER FIRE\nCare Under Fire is the care rendered by fellow soldiers\n(â€œbuddy aidâ€) or the unit medic or corpsman at the scene of the\ninjury while both the immediate responder and the casualty are\nstill under e$ective direct or indirect hostile fire. The primary\nfocus for this phase of field medical care is fire superiority and\nsuppression of the source of ongoing attacks. The only medical\nintervention conducted in this phase is rapid control of ongoing\nhemorrhage, typically by application of a tourniquet and\/or\nhemostatic dressing, which can be self-administered or applied by\na fellow combatant or a combat medic. TACTICAL FIELD CARE\nTactical Field Care is provided by the medic or corpsman once\nno longer under e$ective hostile fire. This phase of care can be\nhighly variable dependent upon the setting, but all e$orts should be\nexpended to minimize the time from injury to arrival at a forward\nmedical treatment facility with surgical capabilities. In addition,\nreengagement with the enemy remains a possibility and must\nalways be anticipated. In this phase of care, the standard critical\nprehospital trauma assessments and interventions are conducted. The new xABCDE approach emphasized in ATLS 11th Edition\nis in alignment with TCCC, which emphasizes hemorrhage\ncontrol (or â€œxâ€) first, followed by airway and breathing.",

    "content" : "In this phase of care, the standard critical\nprehospital trauma assessments and interventions are conducted. The new xABCDE approach emphasized in ATLS 11th Edition\nis in alignment with TCCC, which emphasizes hemorrhage\ncontrol (or â€œxâ€) first, followed by airway and breathing. The most\ncommon cause of potentially preventable deaths on the modern\nbattlefield (up to 90%) is uncontrolled hemorrhage. In this phase\nof care, any additional bleeding is controlled with tourniquets\nor pressure dressings, and resuscitation with available blood\nproducts and tranexamic acid is started. Other interventions that\nare highlighted in this phase include establishing or maintaining\nan airway, decompression of tension pneumothorax, pain control,\nantibiotic administration, and preparation for transport to the\nnext echelon of care. TACTICAL EVACUATION CARE\nTactical Evacuation Care is care rendered once the casualty\nhas been placed in the medical evacuation (MEDEVAC) platform\nand includes care provided from the point of injury and during\ntransport to the most appropriate higher-level medical facility. Care\nin this phase focuses on continuation of the initial interventions\nperformed in the Tactical Field Care phase, assessment and SPECIFIC ADJUNCTS AND TREATMENTS\nCOURSE MANUAL | Chapter 24 | Injury in Combat Zones and Austere Environments\nintervention for any additional life- or limb-threatening injuries,\nand initiation of fluid resuscitation, pain control, and antibiotic\ntherapy (if not already begun). More detailed evaluation and\ngreater options for intervention are indicated in this phase of care,\nwith the caveat that the primary philosophy involves minimizing\nunnecessary or nonurgent interventions and focusing on rapid\ntransportation to a higher level of care. However, experiences in the Ukraine conflict have highlighted\nthe importance of early tourniquet replacement or conversion\nwithin 2 hours of injury to prevent unnecessary limb loss. In cases\nof prolonged tourniquet use, tourniquet conversion or replacement\nmay be accompanied by a systemic post-tourniquet syndrome\nthat includes hypotension, acidosis, and acute renal failure. While tourniquet replacement may occur, tourniquet conversion\nshould not be attempted in patients with profound shock until\nresuscitation has been started. Additionally, tourniquets should\nnever be converted to a pressure dressing in cases of traumatic\namputation until evaluated by a surgeon. FAR FORWARD BLOOD\nIn addition to external hemorrhage control, optimal survival\nin the prehospital setting relies on the availability of adequate\nresuscitation resources. Several military and civilian studies have\nnow suggested a likely mortality benefit for early prehospital\nblood in the bleeding patient. In order to provide blood as near\nto the point of injury as feasible, the US military now maintains\nFDA-compliant, low-titer (anti-A\/anti-B <256), type-O cold-\nstored whole blood, as well as other components (such as dried\nplasma) for use in the field. Additionally, when stored blood products are unavailable, the\nUS military utilizes emergency blood collection (also known as\nâ€œwalking blood bankâ€) to provide fresh whole blood to injured\ncasualties in extremis. Potential donors are screened prior to\ndeployment for blood type, transfusion-transmitted infections,\nand titer so that low-titer, type-O donors are preidentified and\nimmediately available in the field setting when needed. These emergency procedures enable forces in the field to\nprovide life-saving blood transfusions when standard blood\nproducts are unavailable or have been depleted, thereby facilitating\nmovement of the casualty to a damage control surgery capability. Additional information on the procedures and training used for\nemergency fresh whole-blood collection can be found in the Joint\nTrauma System Whole Blood Clinical Practice Guideline or the\nresources listed at the end of the chapter. TOURNIQUETS\nThe immediate arrest of exsanguinating extremity hemorrhage\nwith a tourniquet is the principal life-saving intervention delivered\nin the Care Under Fire stage and is considered the standard of care\nfor the prehospital environment. Because it is frequently di%cult\nto ascertain the exact location of bleeding on an extremity during\nCare Under Fire, TCCC recommends the immediate placement\nof a â€œhigh and tightâ€ tourniquet at the shoulder or groin. During\nlater phases of Tactical Field Care or on arrival to the next echelon\nof care, these hastily placed tourniquets must be assessed for\nreplacement in a location 2â€“3 inches above the wound, conversion\nto a pressure dressing, or removal if not necessary.\nï® Box 24-1: Tourniquet Pitfall and Mitigation. Tourniquet Pitfall and Mitigation\nPitfall Mitigation\nLeaving a â€œhigh and\ntightâ€ tourniquet in\nplace for\nbleeding wound\nTourniquet replacement (2-3â€ above\nwound) or conversion at earliest\nopportunity. Consider\ntourniquet replacement or conversion\nas soon as possible once clinically\nimproved.\nâ€œHigh and tightâ€\ntourniquet placed\nfor traumatic\namputation\nPerform tourniquet replacement once\nshock is improved. ATLS IN THE OPERATIONAL ENVIRONMENT\n(ATLS-OE)\nWhile the widespread adoption of tourniquets in both military\nand civilian settings over the last two decades has been an integral\ncomponent of improving prehospital mortality, studies in both\nsettings have also revealed that up to half of the tourniquets that\nare placed emergently are not necessary for hemorrhage control\nin hindsight.     "content" : "This is especially important following\nan intense and\/or unsuccessful resuscitation. As the trauma team\nleader, it is important to recognize the emotional toll that caring\nfor a severely injured trauma patient takes on all those involved. Planning for structured debriefings to run immediately a\"er the\n283 patient has either stabilized, died, or transferred will allow all\nthose involved to begin to process the potentially overwhelming\nemotions that caring for these patients can bring up. In order to have a team that is ready, willing, and able to care for\nthe next severely injured patient, the trauma team leader should\ntake the time to facilitate these debriefings whenever possible. The\nnature of trauma care may not allow immediate debriefings or for\nall disciplines to always attend; the team leader should engage the\nsystem to arrange for team- and clinician-centered follow-up. Some\nspecialists, such as social workers, spiritual care professionals, and\nmental health counselors, may prove particularly useful. Some\nsites perform routine debriefing that is focused on process or\nquality improvement. While this is critical to optimizing patient\ncare, it is equally important to patient care that clinicians have\nan opportunity to focus on how a resuscitation has emotionally\naffected them. CULTURALLY APPROPRIATE CARE AND\nPROCESSES\nCulturally appropriate care may require the clinician to\nconsider a myriad of aspects, including ethnic heritage and\nreligious traditions, gender identity, and local or even family-\nspecific practices. As mentioned in the introduction, clinicians\nmay need to adapt their approach to communication bearing this\nin mind. They should encourage the family to communicate their\nimportant cultural traditions to the healthcare team. An important\nexample of this would be understanding and incorporating\nculturally appropriate postmortem care practices when a patient\ndies in the acute trauma setting. SPECIAL POPULATION CONSIDERATIONS\nTHE PEDIATRIC PATIENT\nDisclosing to parents the serious injury or death of a child is\nperhaps the most di%cult conversation imaginable. Following\nthe Four Key Concepts and ABCDE communication framework\npresented in the chapter can potentially help mitigate some of the\nintense immediate and long-term psychiatric stress associated\nwith receiving such news. If serious news is disclosed with respect,\nsensitivity, and an expressed understanding of the significance of\nthe news imparted, the parents tend to be grateful for the care\nrendered by the involved sta$. THE PREGNANT TRAUMA PATIENT\nNavigating the disclosure of fetal loss is di%cult for many\nclinicians in the acute postinjury setting but remains a top concern\nfor the pregnant trauma patient. The basic tenets of disclosing\ndeath apply to this scenario. Parents value empathy and kindness. Importantly, terms such as â€œproducts of conception,â€ â€œembryo,â€\nand â€œfetusâ€ are generally not well-received, as parents generally\ntalk about their â€œbabyâ€ from the beginning of pregnancy. Thus,\nthe term â€œbabyâ€ is preferred. If the sex of the baby has been\ndetermined, use of appropriate pronouns is also recommended. 284\nAdvanced Trauma Life SupportÂ® | 11th Edition\nTHE OLDER ADULT\nPatients who are in the later phases of life are more likely than\nother patients to have established beliefs about limitations on care\nat the end of their life, and these should be elicited. Independence,\nrecovery time, and return to functionality are also critical to\nevaluate and communicate if they are known at the time of acute\ntreatment. However, clinicians should be mindful that prognostic\nrecovery is rarely determined by age alone and avoid allowing\nageism to influence their clinical decisions and communication\nwith the care team, patients, and their families. MASS CASUALTY SETTINGS\nIn circumstances where multiple patients present from the\nsame incident, one important consideration is confirmation of\npatient identity before disclosing serious news. In addition, the\nclinician may be called upon to manage multiple concerned\nfamilies simultaneously and must maintain the privacy of each\npatient. Additional details can be found in Chapter 17, Triage and\nDisaster Management. CHAPTER SUMMARY\nProviding updates and having goal-setting discussions with\npatients and families early in the hospital course is an essential\npart of modern, high-quality trauma care. Recognizing that\nthere are many unknowns in the first few hours a\"er injury,\nclinicians should remember that it is not necessary to provide a\ncomprehensive overview of all potential futures. The goal in the\nfirst update is to provide a reasonable assessment of the patientâ€™s\nstatus, support and engage patients and their families as they\nrespond to the injury, and begin to outline treatment goals and any\nlimitations to treatment that the patient may have. The Four Key\nConcepts and the ABCDEs of trauma communication outlined\nin this chapter can provide a useful framework for preparing for,\nimplementing, and processing these conversations. If the patient\nwill be transferred to a di$erent unit or facility, details of these\nconversations, summaries of the patientâ€™s\/familyâ€™s stated goals,\ninformation about surrogate decision-makers, and advanced care\nplans are as important as information about the physical injury\nand should accompany the patient in transport. Patient-\/family-\ncentered trauma care encompasses the whole person and their\nfamily. By engaging in thoughtful, compassionate, and timely\ncommunication with patients and their families, we help them\ncope with what is happening to them in the present and prepare\nfor the future.",

CHAPTER SUMMARY\nTimely assessment and intervention in ocular trauma cases\nare essential. Treatment teams must recognize the signs and\nsymptoms of various ocular conditions, such as chemical injuries,\norbital compartment syndrome, and open globe injuries. Once\nthese three diagnoses have been ruled out or managed, consider\nevaluating and treating other injuries, such as eyelid lacerations\nor orbital fractures. Feel confident assessing and treating ocular\ninjuries; when in doubt, prioritize initial stabilization measures\nand transfer to ophthalmology. 323 ï® Figure 23-6: Ocular Trauma Flowsheet. The recognition of and prompt initialization of treatment for emergent\nocular injuries, such as chemical injuries, ocular compartment syndrome, open globe injuries, and hyphema, can\nhelp prevent vision loss. The initial phases of treatment for these injuries may be accomplished by the emergency\narea clinician while awaiting a formal ophthalmologic consult. 324\nAdvanced Trauma Life SupportÂ® | 11th Edition COURSE MANUAL | Chapter 23 | Ocular Trauma\nKEY LEARNING POINTS\nâ€¢ Ocular injuries have the potential to blind a patient.\nâ€¢ Vision-threatening injuries require treatment\npromptly after onset.\nâ€¢ Perform the ocular exam during the secondary\nsurvey, after stabilizing life- and limb-threatening\ninjuries. Describe the signs of elder abuse and the significance of elder abuse to outcome in geriatric patients Define ageism and how it can impact geriatric trauma care 191 12 Trauma in the Older Adult CHAPTER STATEMENT Geriatric patients represent an increasing demographic among injured patients. Low-energy mechanisms can produce substantial injury. The response of older adults to injury is influenced by preexisting injuries and frailty. Medications and organ dysfunction can alter the presentation of shock. INTRODUCTION Globally, the demographic shi\" toward an aging population is notable, with older adults becoming the fastest-growing age group. By 2050, it is projected that nearly half of the worldâ€™s population will reside in countries where at least 20% are over 60 years old, with a quarter living in countries where older adults make up more than 30% of the populace. This demographic shi\" represents a significant societal change in the 21st century, highlighted by the increased activity levels and longevity of todayâ€™s older adults compared to previous generations. Access to high-quality healthcare in industrialized regions further enhances their quality of life. However, the active lifestyles and increased mobility of the current older adult population also expose them to a greater risk of sustaining serious injuries, making injury the fi\"h leading cause of death among this group. The economic status of older adults a$ects their health, and the health of geriatric patients a$ects the economy. Older adults with lower incomes are more likely to have disabilities and die younger. For those with lower incomes, disability is likely to begin earlier in life, further increasing the risk of early mortality. The social determinants of health play a key role in the health trajectory of older adults. Older adults have greater healthcare and long-term care needs than younger people, leading to increased healthcare expenditures. 192 Advanced Trauma Life SupportÂ® | 11th Edition Managing geriatric trauma patients presents distinct challenges for trauma care teams. The injury mechanisms in older adults differ, with falls being more prevalent, followed closely by motor vehicle and motorcycle crashes. Inadequate triage of critically injured geriatric patients may contribute to their higher mortality rates compared to younger individuals. Beyond triage issues, the aging of organ systems, preexisting medical conditions, and frailty significantly elevate the mortality risk in older adults, underscoring that age alone cannot predict recovery potential. Mental health issues, substance abuse, and the risk of maltreatment are additional considerations. Achieving the best outcomes for geriatric trauma patients demands a comprehensive, multidisciplinary approach focused on meticulous initial assessment, e$ective resuscitation, and continuous care. This strategy emphasizes the need for heightened vigilance and tailored interventions to address the unique vulnerabilities of the aging population in the trauma setting. EFFECTS OF AGING Aging is inherently associated with a decline in cellular function, leading to diminished organ performance. This decline is marked by a decrease in the bodyâ€™s ability to adapt and maintain homeostasis, resulting in a heightened vulnerability to injury-related stress, a phenomenon o\"en referred to as \"reduced physiological reserve\" (ï® Table 12-1). Consequently, injuries that younger individuals might easily recover from can have severe, sometimes catastrophic, outcomes in older adults. Research has documented the significant impact of preexisting conditions (PECs) on the morbidity and mortality outcomes of trauma patients. A notable study identified five critical PECsâ€” cirrhosis, coagulopathy, chronic obstructive pulmonary disease, ischemic heart disease, and diabetes mellitusâ€”that significantly influence trauma patient outcomes. In an analysis involving over 3,000 patients, it was found that one in four individuals aged 65 or older had at least one of these conditions. Furthermore, the presence of one or more of the critical PECs nearly doubled the mortality risk compared to patients without such conditions. The study also highlighted the interplay between injury severity and host factors, including age, gender, and PECs, underscoring that while injury severity is a primary mortality determinant, host factors are also crucial (ï® Figures 12-1A and B).",

    "content" : "5. Describe the signs of elder abuse and the significance of elder abuse to outcome in geriatric patients Define ageism and how it can impact geriatric trauma care 191 12 Trauma in the Older Adult CHAPTER STATEMENT Geriatric patients represent an increasing demographic among injured patients. Low-energy mechanisms can produce substantial injury. The response of older adults to injury is influenced by preexisting injuries and frailty. Medications and organ dysfunction can alter the presentation of shock. INTRODUCTION Globally, the demographic shi\" toward an aging population is notable, with older adults becoming the fastest-growing age group. By 2050, it is projected that nearly half of the worldâ€™s population will reside in countries where at least 20% are over 60 years old, with a quarter living in countries where older adults make up more than 30% of the populace. This demographic shi\" represents a significant societal change in the 21st century, highlighted by the increased activity levels and longevity of todayâ€™s older adults compared to previous generations. Access to high-quality healthcare in industrialized regions further enhances their quality of life. However, the active lifestyles and increased mobility of the current older adult population also expose them to a greater risk of sustaining serious injuries, making injury the fi\"h leading cause of death among this group. The economic status of older adults a$ects their health, and the health of geriatric patients a$ects the economy. Older adults with lower incomes are more likely to have disabilities and die younger. For those with lower incomes, disability is likely to begin earlier in life, further increasing the risk of early mortality. The social determinants of health play a key role in the health trajectory of older adults. Older adults have greater healthcare and long-term care needs than younger people, leading to increased healthcare expenditures. 192 Advanced Trauma Life SupportÂ® | 11th Edition Managing geriatric trauma patients presents distinct challenges for trauma care teams. The injury mechanisms in older adults differ, with falls being more prevalent, followed closely by motor vehicle and motorcycle crashes. Inadequate triage of critically injured geriatric patients may contribute to their higher mortality rates compared to younger individuals. Beyond triage issues, the aging of organ systems, preexisting medical conditions, and frailty significantly elevate the mortality risk in older adults, underscoring that age alone cannot predict recovery potential. Mental health issues, substance abuse, and the risk of maltreatment are additional considerations. Achieving the best outcomes for geriatric trauma patients demands a comprehensive, multidisciplinary approach focused on meticulous initial assessment, e$ective resuscitation, and continuous care. This strategy emphasizes the need for heightened vigilance and tailored interventions to address the unique vulnerabilities of the aging population in the trauma setting. EFFECTS OF AGING Aging is inherently associated with a decline in cellular function, leading to diminished organ performance. This decline is marked by a decrease in the bodyâ€™s ability to adapt and maintain homeostasis, resulting in a heightened vulnerability to injury-related stress, a phenomenon o\"en referred to as \"reduced physiological reserve\" (ï® Table 12-1). Consequently, injuries that younger individuals might easily recover from can have severe, sometimes catastrophic, outcomes in older adults. Research has documented the significant impact of preexisting conditions (PECs) on the morbidity and mortality outcomes of trauma patients. A notable study identified five critical PECsâ€” cirrhosis, coagulopathy, chronic obstructive pulmonary disease, ischemic heart disease, and diabetes mellitusâ€”that significantly influence trauma patient outcomes. In an analysis involving over 3,000 patients, it was found that one in four individuals aged 65 or older had at least one of these conditions. Furthermore, the presence of one or more of the critical PECs nearly doubled the mortality risk compared to patients without such conditions. The study also highlighted the interplay between injury severity and host factors, including age, gender, and PECs, underscoring that while injury severity is a primary mortality determinant, host factors are also crucial (ï® Figures 12-1A and B).",

Describe the signs of elder abuse and the significance of elder abuse to outcome in geriatric patients Define ageism and how it can impact geriatric trauma care 191 12 Trauma in the Older Adult CHAPTER STATEMENT Geriatric patients represent an increasing demographic among injured patients. Low-energy mechanisms can produce substantial injury. The response of older adults to injury is influenced by preexisting injuries and frailty. Medications and organ dysfunction can alter the presentation of shock. INTRODUCTION Globally, the demographic shi\" toward an aging population is notable, with older adults becoming the fastest-growing age group. By 2050, it is projected that nearly half of the worldâ€™s population will reside in countries where at least 20% are over 60 years old, with a quarter living in countries where older adults make up more than 30% of the populace. This demographic shi\" represents a significant societal change in the 21st century, highlighted by the increased activity levels and longevity of todayâ€™s older adults compared to previous generations. Access to high-quality healthcare in industrialized regions further enhances their quality of life. However, the active lifestyles and increased mobility of the current older adult population also expose them to a greater risk of sustaining serious injuries, making injury the fi\"h leading cause of death among this group. The economic status of older adults a$ects their health, and the health of geriatric patients a$ects the economy. Older adults with lower incomes are more likely to have disabilities and die younger. For those with lower incomes, disability is likely to begin earlier in life, further increasing the risk of early mortality. The social determinants of health play a key role in the health trajectory of older adults. Older adults have greater healthcare and long-term care needs than younger people, leading to increased healthcare expenditures. 192 Advanced Trauma Life SupportÂ® | 11th Edition Managing geriatric trauma patients presents distinct challenges for trauma care teams. The injury mechanisms in older adults differ, with falls being more prevalent, followed closely by motor vehicle and motorcycle crashes. Inadequate triage of critically injured geriatric patients may contribute to their higher mortality rates compared to younger individuals. Beyond triage issues, the aging of organ systems, preexisting medical conditions, and frailty significantly elevate the mortality risk in older adults, underscoring that age alone cannot predict recovery potential. Mental health issues, substance abuse, and the risk of maltreatment are additional considerations. Achieving the best outcomes for geriatric trauma patients demands a comprehensive, multidisciplinary approach focused on meticulous initial assessment, e$ective resuscitation, and continuous care. This strategy emphasizes the need for heightened vigilance and tailored interventions to address the unique vulnerabilities of the aging population in the trauma setting. EFFECTS OF AGING Aging is inherently associated with a decline in cellular function, leading to diminished organ performance. This decline is marked by a decrease in the bodyâ€™s ability to adapt and maintain homeostasis, resulting in a heightened vulnerability to injury-related stress, a phenomenon o\"en referred to as \"reduced physiological reserve\" (ï® Table 12-1). Consequently, injuries that younger individuals might easily recover from can have severe, sometimes catastrophic, outcomes in older adults. Research has documented the significant impact of preexisting conditions (PECs) on the morbidity and mortality outcomes of trauma patients. A notable study identified five critical PECsâ€” cirrhosis, coagulopathy, chronic obstructive pulmonary disease, ischemic heart disease, and diabetes mellitusâ€”that significantly influence trauma patient outcomes. In an analysis involving over 3,000 patients, it was found that one in four individuals aged 65 or older had at least one of these conditions. Furthermore, the presence of one or more of the critical PECs nearly doubled the mortality risk compared to patients without such conditions. The study also highlighted the interplay between injury severity and host factors, including age, gender, and PECs, underscoring that while injury severity is a primary mortality determinant, host factors are also crucial (ï® Figures 12-1A and B).",

  {
    "content" : "As the trauma team leader, it is important to recognize the emotional toll that caring for a severely injured trauma patient takes on all those involved. Planning for structured debriefings to run immediately a\"er the 283 patient has either stabilized, died, or transferred will allow all those involved to begin to process the potentially overwhelming emotions that caring for these patients can bring up. In order to have a team that is ready, willing, and able to care for the next severely injured patient, the trauma team leader should take the time to facilitate these debriefings whenever possible. The nature of trauma care may not allow immediate debriefings or for all disciplines to always attend; the team leader should engage the system to arrange for team- and clinician-centered follow-up. Some specialists, such as social workers, spiritual care professionals, and mental health counselors, may prove particularly useful. Some sites perform routine debriefing that is focused on process or quality improvement. While this is critical to optimizing patient care, it is equally important to patient care that clinicians have an opportunity to focus on how a resuscitation has emotionally affected them. CULTURALLY APPROPRIATE CARE AND PROCESSES Culturally appropriate care may require the clinician to consider a myriad of aspects, including ethnic heritage and religious traditions, gender identity, and local or even familyspecific practices. As mentioned in the introduction, clinicians may need to adapt their approach to communication bearing this in mind. They should encourage the family to communicate their important cultural traditions to the healthcare team. An important example of this would be understanding and incorporating culturally appropriate postmortem care practices when a patient dies in the acute trauma setting. SPECIAL POPULATION CONSIDERATIONS THE PEDIATRIC PATIENT Disclosing to parents the serious injury or death of a child is perhaps the most di%cult conversation imaginable. Following the Four Key Concepts and ABCDE communication framework presented in the chapter can potentially help mitigate some of the intense immediate and long-term psychiatric stress associated with receiving such news. If serious news is disclosed with respect, sensitivity, and an expressed understanding of the significance of the news imparted, the parents tend to be grateful for the care rendered by the involved sta$. THE PREGNANT TRAUMA PATIENT Navigating the disclosure of fetal loss is di%cult for many clinicians in the acute postinjury setting but remains a top concern for the pregnant trauma patient. The basic tenets of disclosing death apply to this scenario. Parents value empathy and kindness. Importantly, terms such as â€œproducts of conception,â€ â€œembryo,â€ and â€œfetusâ€ are generally not well-received, as parents generally talk about their â€œbabyâ€ from the beginning of pregnancy. Thus, the term â€œbabyâ€ is preferred. If the sex of the baby has been determined, use of appropriate pronouns is also recommended. 284 Advanced Trauma Life SupportÂ® | 11th Edition THE OLDER ADULT Patients who are in the later phases of life are more likely than other patients to have established beliefs about limitations on care at the end of their life, and these should be elicited. Independence, recovery time, and return to functionality are also critical to evaluate and communicate if they are known at the time of acute treatment. However, clinicians should be mindful that prognostic recovery is rarely determined by age alone and avoid allowing ageism to influence their clinical decisions and communication with the care team, patients, and their families. MASS CASUALTY SETTINGS In circumstances where multiple patients present from the same incident, one important consideration is confirmation of patient identity before disclosing serious news. In addition, the clinician may be called upon to manage multiple concerned families simultaneously and must maintain the privacy of each patient. Additional details can be found in Chapter 17, Triage and Disaster Management. CHAPTER SUMMARY Providing updates and having goal-setting discussions with patients and families early in the hospital course is an essential part of modern, high-quality trauma care. Recognizing that there are many unknowns in the first few hours a\"er injury, clinicians should remember that it is not necessary to provide a comprehensive overview of all potential futures. The goal in the first update is to provide a reasonable assessment of the patientâ€™s status, support and engage patients and their families as they respond to the injury, and begin to outline treatment goals and any limitations to treatment that the patient may have. The Four Key Concepts and the ABCDEs of trauma communication outlined in this chapter can provide a useful framework for preparing for, implementing, and processing these conversations. If the patient will be transferred to a di$erent unit or facility, details of these conversations, summaries of the patientâ€™s\/familyâ€™s stated goals, information about surrogate decision-makers, and advanced care plans are as important as information about the physical injury and should accompany the patient in transport.",
    "id" : "11c2a601-7181-4aed-a19c-226b76cd66a3",
    "page_number" : 94,
    "similarity" : 0.48642278635144198

    "content" : "If interpreters are necessary, they should be professionally trained, as opposed to being other family members; this ensures correct translation of any medical terminology and removes stress from the family member who might act as the interpreter in less formal settings. 281 ï® Figure 20-2: Preparing to Meet With a Family or Patient. Using a checklist, like that illustrated in this Figure, can help the clinician prepare for the meeting. Some clinicians may be concerned about the safety of their team as well as the family. The suddenness of the traumatic event and hearing serious news may catch the family o$ guard or unprepared. The information o\"en generates intense emotional reactions that may range from shock to anger. Having a strategy to exit should the physical space become unsafe is useful. For example, keeping the door to the room open and unblocked when delivering news to a large group and sitting close to the door may help. Clinicians should work with their institutions to develop protocols to manage such situations. KEY CONCEPT #3: MEETING\/ COMMUNICATING WITH THE FAMILY Tyrie et al describe an â€œABCDEâ€ communication algorithm that may be used during a family meeting at which serious news is discussed: A= Acquaint yourself with the family\/Ask A good way to begin the conversation a\"er making introductions is to ask an open-ended question, such as, â€œWhat have you been told about what has been going on?â€ Allowing the family to describe in their own words their understanding will provide a foundation for ongoing discussion. This is an opportunity for the clinician to mainly listen. It may also be appropriate at this â€œAskâ€ stage to ascertain if the patient has an existing advance directive or if they have communicated any wishes regarding life-sustaining therapy B = Begin with a warning As mentioned above, starting the â€œHeadlineâ€ statement with â€œI have serious news to share with youâ€ alerts the family and sets the stage for the conversation. C = Concise summary When delivering serious news, clinicians may be anxious and revert to communicating using a plethora of details and medical jargon. This style of communication can be overwhelming and incomprehensible to the patient\/family such that the main message gets lost in translation. This is why prior preparation of the â€œHeadlineâ€ statement is so useful. Particularly when the news may be associated with poor prognosis, â€œheadlineâ€ the most serious injury. Resist the tendency to catalog every injury in the initial meeting; family will need time to digest information. Focus on the â€œFactâ€ and especially the â€œMeaningâ€ of the most serious diagnosis and how this injury will impact the patientâ€™s bigpicture condition. Do they have brain damage or paralysis? Will they survive? Clinicians should try to avoid being overly optimistic or paint information more favorably than is realistic when communicating serious news and poor outcomes. Keep in mind that attempts to so\"en the message may result in inadvertent miscommunication, which can itself result in mistrust between patients\/families and clinicians. The goal is to provide the truth, as far as it is known at that time, about the nature of the patientâ€™s injuries and anticipated course, regardless of how hard it is to hear. When communicating news of death, recognize that there is no way to so\"en this news; maintaining clarity with use of the â€œDâ€ word (died, dead) is extremely important. Communicate news of the death first a\"er a brief narrative (one sentence) that provides context that the family can understand, and then add other details. Like any serious news, disclosures of death should be preceded with a warning. A sample script may look something like, â€œI have very serious news to share with you. Your ____ was in a car crash with major injuries to his heart and brain. We tried everything we could; he died a few minutes agoâ€¦.â€ 282 Advanced Trauma Life SupportÂ® | 11th Edition",

It is the fluidity and potential chaos inherent to the austere environment that dictate the importance of the Zero Survey in practice. Triage decisions and initial care priorities may change rapidly as the situational factors and care capacity of the facility evolve over time and between events. In this environment, as personnel and supply resources become more limited, triage decisions become increasingly di%cult. QUATERNARY SURVEY Although the standard ATLS Course emphasizes preparation of the injured patient for transfer from the initial facility to a trauma center, this is typically a single transfer over a relatively short distance by a fully equipped medical team. In contrast, a patient in the operational environment may undergo multiple sequential transfers over prolonged distances, while initial resuscitation is ongoing. It is not uncommon to have a patient undergo a major damage control surgery and then be placed into the medical evacuation continuum within minutes to hours of surgery and\/or injury. These transfers may be via helicopter, ground ambulance, or even watercra\", making ongoing, enroute care exceedingly challenging. Therefore, strict attention must be paid to the complete preparation of the patient for safe transportation in order to minimize the likelihood of problems or complications arising during transport. The Quaternary Survey formalizes this preparation for transfer. It should be repeated for each successive transfer in the medical evacuation chain. In the operational setting, the time in transit may be a matter of minutes or may be many hours, and this must also be factored into not only the preparation for transport but also the decision about readiness for transport. Enroute care capabilities must also be considered, because they may vary widely in terms of the transportation platform, available enroute care providers, equipment, supplies and medications, environment, and the potential for external threats. Assessing the patientâ€™s response to resuscitation is critical. The potential of meeting desirable end points of resuscitation versus the local resources to meet these end points are real and important considerations. Although it is certainly desirable to ensure that a severely injured patient is clinically stable, has had a complete and thorough evaluation with identification of all injuries, and has been fully resuscitated to standard end points, this is o\"en not COURSE MANUAL | Chapter 24 | Injury in Combat Zones and Austere Environments practical or possible in the operational environment. The limited supply of critical resources such as blood products, as well as the limited holding capacity of the most forward medical treatment facilities (such as the Forward Surgical Team), make prolonged care and sustained massive transfusions logistically impossible. Thus, o\"en the best of two suboptimal choices must be made, and the patient is placed into the transport system at a much earlier phase or in a more tenuous point of their resuscitation than is frequently done in the civilian setting. Additional considerations as patients are moved in the operational environment include the following: â€¢ Will weather or hostile action prevent movement of casualties? â€¢ What supportive treatments must accompany the patient (ventilator, suction), and what are the potential enroute problems or malfunctions that could occur? â€¢ Will the evacuation team have the skills to manage a critically ill patient and the supportive equipment accompanying the patient? â€¢ What medications, fluids, blood products, and other resuscitative or supportive treatments can realistically and reliably be administered during the transport? â€¢ What protective equipment is needed to prevent hypothermia, eye injury, and ear\/hearing injury during transport?",

    "content" : "Finance\/Administration â€¢ Monitor costs, execute contracts, provide legal advice. â€¢ Maintain records of personnel. 254 Advanced Trauma Life SupportÂ® | 11th Edition The structure of the ICS is the same regardless of the disaster; the di$erence is in the particular expertise of key personnel. An important part of hospital disaster planning is to identify the incident commander and other key positions before a disaster occurs. The positions should be sta$ed 24 hours a day, 7 days a week during a disaster. Each person in the command structure should supervise only 3â€“7 persons. This approach is significantly di$erent from conventional hospital management. All medical clinicians must be aware of and adhere to the ICS structure to ensure that they integrate successfully into the disaster response. Clinician safety is paramount during an MCI. In the field, situational awareness is critical to avoid becoming a casualty oneself. There are many hazards to watch for, including vehicular accidents, trips and falls, collapsed or damaged structures, cuts and burns, infectious disease, smoke, CBRN (chemical, biological, radiological, nuclear) exposure, unsecured weapons, explosives, bad weather (lightning, swi\" water, heat and cold), exposed or damaged electrical and gas lines, and hostile activity. Proper personal protective equipment (PPE) is required. This may be nonmedical, such as work gloves, rubber gloves, hard hats, boots, goggles, and head lamps, or medical, such as surgical gloves, masks (such as N95 or higher respiratory protection with chemical filtering), and impermeable coveralls. If radiation is known or expected, responders should have personal dosimeters to assess time permitted on scene and to provide confirmation of inconsequential exposure. At the hospital, all casualties suspected of being contaminated with biologic toxins, hazardous chemicals, or radioactive particles should be decontaminated before entering the emergency department. Otherwise, the facility becomes contaminated and dangerous for patients, clinicians, visitors, and other casualties. Surface decontamination at a minimum requires removal of clothing and washing with soap and water. Lifesaving measures such as hemorrhage control, chest decompression, airway control, and administration of antidotes can be done at any stage of care, including before decontamination. However, clinicians must wear appropriate PPE. A\"er decontamination, standard contact precautions are appropriate. Finally, consideration should always be given to the possibility of a MEI. What if there is so much damage to healthcare infrastructure that medical care cannot be provided? Tornados have destroyed hospitals here in the US, earthquakes have destroyed hospitals from Haiti to China, and warâ€”the ultimate human-caused disasterâ€”has destroyed entire countriesâ€™ healthcare systems. Such catastrophes may require evacuation of casualties out of the disaster zone or transport of entire field hospitals into the disaster zone. Evacuation procedures should be part of every hospitalâ€™s disaster plan. More information about this topic can be found in the DMEP Course. COURSE MANUAL | Chapter 17 | Triage and Disaster Management CHAPTER SUMMARY This chapter introduces the ATLS learner to the principles of triage, prioritization of care, and allocation of resources as related to mass casualty situations. It also summarizes the phases of disaster management, the ICS, and safety concepts, both at the scene and at the hospital. Finally, it asks the learner to consider what should be done in a catastrophic situation with no available resources. Mastering all these concepts requires years of training and experience, but this chapter and the accompanying triage scenarios provide at least a starting point. The next steps suggested include DMEP and other courses, which can be found via the links provided below. Describe and demonstrate principles of allocation of resources in the ER and the OR during MCIs Describe approaches to clinician safety in disaster management Describe the structure and principles of Incident Command Identify challenges for mass casualty care in austere or resource-constrained environments 249 17 Triage and Disaster Management CHAPTER STATEMENT The care of the individual injured patient must be expanded in a mass casualty incident or disaster situation. E$ective application of ATLSÂ® and triage principles are essential. In addition, trauma clinicians need to be familiar with disaster management principles, the Incident Command System, and unusual threats to safety. Finally, they should be prepared to function in austere conditions. INTRODUCTION Mass casualty and disaster management are very broad topics that require years of training and experience to master. This chapter will introduce you to the principles of triage, prioritization of care, and allocation of resources as they relate to mass casualty situations. Mass casualty incidents require a change in concept from individual patient care to population-based care, and we will show how ATLSÂ® principles apply to disaster management as compared to treatment of individual patients. The phases of disaster management will be summarized here but are described in detail in the Disaster Management and Emergency Preparedness (DMEPÂ®) course, which is now available online. We will discuss the Incident Command System (ICS), a very common and important standardized approach to disaster management that is o\"en not taught in medical education or surgical practice. Special attention also needs to be given to clinician safety, not just from the usual suspects such as needlesticks and blood-borne pathogens but also from chemical, biological, and radiological threats, and even from direct physical attack. Finally, we will discuss the challenges of mass casualty care in austere environments, whether in a low-income country or in a community that has just su$ered catastrophic damage. First, letâ€™s define a few terms. A mass casualty event (MCE) is a situation that generates multiple injured patients who stretch, but do not overwhelm, patient care resources. Resources may already be present or rapidly mobilized so that each injured patient receives individual care that follows the full ATLS paradigm. The number of simultaneous injured patients that constitutes an MCE varies by healthcare facility and community; there is more tolerance of multiple injured patients in urban settings. A mass casualty incident (MCI) generates a large number of casualties with needs that exceed available healthcare resources and management systems for individual patient care. The mass casualty threshold varies between healthcare facilities and systems. 250 Advanced Trauma Life SupportÂ® | 11th Edition A mass event incident (MEI) is a situation that disrupts the capabilities of the normal healthcare system while also generating a large of number of casualties. If a healthcare facility is partially or completely destroyed, both the existing patients and new casualties must be relocated. These patients become an additional group in the overall casualty population. An MEI may result in no healthcare resources being available for the initial or extended response. Thus, an MEI is a catastrophic MCI. The transition points across this continuum will vary based on the situation, number and type of casualties, and available resources. The fundamental challenge in an MCI is that casualty need exceeds resources that we normally use for individualized care. When resources are scarce, a decision made for one of the injured impacts what can be done for every other injured person. The focus therefore must shi\" from â€œgreatest good for the individual injured patientâ€ to â€œgreatest good for the casualty population.â€ Moving from an individual patient mindset to a mass casualty mindset may seem easy to apply in theory, but it is quite di%cult in practice. So how does one apply the concept of â€œthe greatest good for the casualty populationâ€ in mass casualty situations? Mass casualty response is shaped by three dynamic variables: situation, casualties, and resources. The goals in mass casualty management are quick identification of and initiation of care for critically injured, salvageable casualties, and keeping clin

    "content" : "A prominent injury pattern during the Iraq and Afghanistan conflicts, known as dismounted complex blast injury, includes multiple traumatic amputations, pelvic injury, and traumatic brain injury (ï® Table 24-2). Common Blast Injury Patterns Seen on the Battlefield Blast mechanism Common injuries Primary Cause of Mortality Mounted Closed extremity fractures, spine fractures, traumatic brain injury Traumatic brain injury Dismounted, low energy Open and closed extremity fractures, amputations, multiple open wounds, vascular injury Hemorrhage Dismounted, high energy Multiple traumatic amputations, open and closed pelvic fractures, spine fractures, traumatic brain injury, crush injury Traumatic brain injury, hemorrhage, delayed complications of wounds or multiorgan failure 329 Low-energy explosives, such as gunpowder in pipe bombs or pressure cookers, tend to produce mostly secondary blast injuries from fragments in a smaller radius; however, those close to such explosions may have primary blast injury, extensive penetrations, and amputations. Initial management includes immediate hemorrhage control and resuscitation, followed by early wound irrigation of contamination and debridement of devitalized tissue. One must maintain a hypervigilant index of suspicion for internal damage, including vascular injuries, because high-energy blasts o\"en result in a complex combination of wounds, ranging from devastating traumatic amputation to multiple small penetrating wounds with highly variable penetration and wound trajectories that are extremely di%cult to assess without adjunct imaging. RADIATION AND CHEMICAL AGENTS Radiological and chemical agents may also be involved in conflict, industrial accidents, terrorist attacks, or as a consequence of natural disasters, such as at Fukushima. Deployed medical units must provide means to detect these agents and decontaminate patients as needed prior to assessing and performing initial management of associated injuries. The US Department of Health and Human Services Administration for Strategic Preparedness and Responseâ€™s Radiation Emergency Medical Management (REMM) and Chemical Hazards Emergency Medical Management (CHEMM) websites and apps are good planning resources for radiation and chemical exposure management. Further complicating matters is the fact that when these agents are used in warfare, the injury pattern o\"en includes not only the insult from the radiation or toxin but also other aspects of polytrauma and burn injury. Depending upon the toxin and degree of exposure, this may impact the anticipated prognosis and treatment options. SYSTEM CONSIDERATIONS JOINT TRAUMA SYSTEM AND TACTICAL COMBAT CASUALTY CARE Mortality from combat wounds steadily improved over the course of the wars in Iraq and Afghanistan despite an increase in Injury Severity Scores. This trend stems from improvements in protective equipment, establishment of a system of in-theater trauma care, use of tourniquets, application of damage control surgery and resuscitation, and use of long-range critical care transport for the most severely injured. This was only possible due to the widespread implementation of Tactical Combat Casualty Care (TCCC) and the establishment of the US Department of Defenseâ€™s Joint Trauma System (JTS), which supports trauma readiness and outcomes through evidence-driven performance improvement. Today, through maintenance of the Department of Defense Trauma Registry, JTS Clinical Practice Guidelines, and TCCC guidelines, the JTS ensures high-quality trauma care throughout the US militaryâ€™s globally deployed trauma system. Additional resources including guidelines for deployed trauma care can be found on the JTS website (https:\/\/jts.health.mil\/). 330 Advanced Trauma Life SupportÂ® | 11th Edition A precedent for the modification of civilian trauma training courses to incorporate military-specific needs can be found in the example of Prehospital Trauma Life Support (PHTLS) and TCCC. Initially developed as a curriculum for US Special Operations Command, TCCC has now been implemented across the battlefield and is the standard for combat prehospital care. A Military Edition of the PHTLS textbook was developed to help support this curriculum. The widespread implementation and training of all combat personnel in TCCC as competent initial responders has resulted in demonstrable reductions in preventable death on the battlefield. TCCC divides point of injury care into three distinct phases: 1. Care Under Fire 2. Tactical Field Care 3. Tactical Evacuation CARE UNDER FIRE Care Under Fire is the care rendered by fellow soldiers (â€œbuddy aidâ€) or the unit medic or corpsman at the scene of the injury while both the immediate responder and the casualty are still under e$ective direct or indirect hostile fire. The primary focus for this phase of field medical care is fire superiority and suppression of the source of ongoing attacks. The only medical intervention conducted in this phase is rapid control of ongoing hemorrhage, typically by application of a tourniquet and\/or hemostatic dressing, which can be self-administered or applied by a fellow combatant or a combat medic. TACTICAL FIELD CARE Tactical Field Care is provided by the medic or corpsman once no longer under e$ective hostile fire.",

Practical frameworks such as the Three Es are readily available to support trauma care clinicians in gathering injury context information, providing a careful hando$ during patient transfer, and identifying locally relevant injury prevention priorities. Clinicians are in a privileged role that provides unique and valuable information that can be used to prevent future injuries at both the individual and population levels. KEY LEARNING POINTS â€¢ Remember that trauma\/injury is NO accident! Most trauma and injuries are preventable. â€¢ Front-line trauma clinicians are in a unique position to contribute to injury prevention efforts locally, regionally, and nationally. â€¢ Learn about the injury context for patients once it is safe and feasible to do so. â€¢ Collecting injury data can be helpful to inform local and national prevention efforts. â€¢ If your facility encounters a high volume of a certain type of injury mechanism or treats a patient population that is particularly impacted by injury, consider targeted injury-prevention interventions. â€¢ When screening for risk factors, clinicians should ensure that options for referral are available to the patient. This figure illustrates the predicted increased mortality risk in older adults based on age and the number of preexisting medical conditions. Patients with multiple preexisting conditions have a significantly higher predicted risk of mortality. A. Represents the mortality risk in men. B. Represents the mortality risk in women. A. B. Risk of Morality-Associated Geriatric Complications or Death, by Age and Number of Preexisting Conditions: Used with permission of Wolters Kluwer Health, Inc., from Min L, Burruss S, Morley E, et al. A simple clinical risk nomogram to predict mortality-associated geriatric complications in severely injured geriatric patients. J Trauma Acute Care Surg. 2013;74(4):1125-1132; permission conveyed through Copyright Clearance Center, Inc. The concept of frailty in the older adult, characterized by a diminished response to physiological stress due to lower physiological reserve and multiorgan system insu%ciency, has been recognized for decades and has more recently been applied to the trauma population. The development of a Trauma-Specific Frailty Index (ï® Figure 12-2) has enabled healthcare clinicians to identify patients at greater risk of poor outcomes and with increased resource needs. Research indicates that targeted interventions for these frail geriatric patients can lead to improved outcomes, emphasizing the importance of a tailored approach in the management of geriatric trauma patients. 194 Advanced Trauma Life SupportÂ® | 11th Edition MECHANISM OF INJURY Older adults are experience common mechanisms of injury such as motor vehicle crash (MVC), being struck as a pedestrian, burns, and penetrating injuries. Additionallyâ€”and somewhat unique to older adultsâ€”is the frequency of injury from groundlevel falls, which can result in severe injury despite a seemingly trivial mechanism. FALLS The likelihood of experiencing a fall increase with age, and falls are the leading cause of fatal injury among older adults. Many factors heighten the risk of falls, including older age, physical limitations, a history of falls, the use of certain medications, dementia, balance issues, and gait instability, as well as visual, cognitive, and neurological impairments. Environmental hazards such as loose rugs, inadequate lighting, and slippery or irregular flooring also contribute significantly to the risk. Falls can also result from syncope or occult infection. Despite the seemingly minor nature of ground-level falls, they can inflict severe, life-threatening injuries in older adults due to underlying frailty, osteoporosis, existing chronic conditions, and the use of certain medications such as anticoagulants. Traumatic brain injuries (TBI) stand out as a common injury from falls among older adults. Fi\"y-one percent of TBIs in older adults are caused by falls, while MVCs cause 9%. Additionally, falls leading to fractures, especially hip fractures, frequently result in a significant reduction of independence for nearly half of the geriatric patients affected. MOTOR VEHICLE\/MOTORCYCLE COLLISION MVCs and motorcycle collisions (MCCs) rank as the secondand third-most common causes of injuries among older adults. Typically, older individuals drive fewer miles and at lower speeds compared to younger drivers.",

COURSE MANUAL | Chapter 24 | Injury in Combat Zones and Austere Environments ï® Table 24-1: Comparison of Factors Impacting Trauma Care. Comparison of Factors Impacting Trauma Care in the Civilian Urban, Civilian Rural, and Operational\/Disaster Environments Civilian Urban Civilian Rural Operational\/Disaster Threat level None None High Resources Readily available May be limited Severely limited Personnel Excess Limited but expandable Fixed and limited Supplies\/ Equipment Fully equipped, resupply readily available Adequately equipped, delay to resupply Limited supplies, resupply significantly delayed Available Expertise Full subspecialty services Limited specialties locally available No subspecialty services immediately available Transfer Availability Immediately available Available but longer transport times Highly variable, may be no option for transfer Multiple or Mass Casualty Events Uncommon Rare Common SPECIFIC INJURY MECHANISMS WAR WOUNDS It is important to consider the unique wounding patterns associated with weapons of war, such as injuries resulting from high-velocity firearms and high-energy explosives. High-energy gunshot wounds, such as those from assault rifles, are created by the linear and cavitating (radial) energy of the missile and cause tissue devitalization and destruction outside the actual path of the missile. High-energy explosives are commonly found in military munitions or in the commercial-grade explosives o\"en seen in improvised explosive devices (IEDs). Explosions from these ï® Table 24-2: Common Blast Injury Patterns Seen on the Battlefield. weapons cause multidimensional blast injuries across four mechanisms: primary blast from the supersonic pressure wave; secondary blast from fragments; tertiary blast from blunt or penetrating impact in the environment; and quaternary blast from burns, crush injuries, or infections. While IEDs are most o\"en encountered in theaters of war, they are also increasingly utilized as a weapon of choice for intentional mass casualty events at home and abroad. Mortality and morbidity depend upon the distance from the device, extent of available protection, and proximity to immediate medical support. A prominent injury pattern during the Iraq and Afghanistan conflicts, known as dismounted complex blast injury, includes multiple traumatic amputations, pelvic injury, and traumatic brain injury (ï® Table 24-2).
